The story of Hillel on the roof is an exemplum: it does not establish a legal precedent (though it has legal implications) so much as it provides an ideal of devotion to Torah that the student is invited to emulate.

Other classic exempla:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבן יוחנן בן זכאי שהיה רוכב על החמור והיה מהלך בדרך, ורבי אלעזר בן ערך מחמר אחריו. אמר לו: רבי, שנה לי פרק אחד במעשה מרכבה</span>
>
> *It happened with Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai that he was riding on a donkey and traveling on the road, and Rabbi Elazar ben Arakh was driving behind him. He said to him: Master, teach me a chapter of the Chariot.*[^47]

The story that follows — in which Rabbi Elazar expounds the mysteries of the divine chariot while fire descends from heaven and the trees sing — is an exemplum of inspired teaching, of the moment when rhetorical performance rises to a supernatural level.

**3. The Conflict Narrative**

This type tells the story of a dispute between sages, preserving the arguments on both sides. The narrative of the Oven of Akhnai (Bava Metzia 59b) — which we will examine many times in this book — is the most famous conflict narrative in rabbinic literature. Rabbi Eliezer marshals every form of persuasion, including miracles and a heavenly voice, but the majority rules against him on the principle that "the Torah is not in heaven."[^48]

Conflict narratives are valuable for rhetorical training because they preserve both sides of a dispute. The student can analyze the arguments deployed by each party, evaluate their relative strength, and consider how the narrative's resolution depends on implicit assumptions about the nature of authority, evidence, and persuasion.

**4. The Biographical Narrative**

This type preserves details about a sage's life — his origins, his training, his relationships, his death. The biographical narratives in Avot de-Rabbi Natan, in particular, provide rich character portraits that serve as the basis for the more advanced rhetorical exercise of encomium (praise), which we will study in Part VII.

### The Virtues of Narrative

The Greek rhetoricians identified three "virtues" (qualities of excellence) that every narrative should possess: **clarity**, **brevity**, and **plausibility**.[^49] These three criteria provide a useful standard for evaluating any narrative, including the narratives you compose yourself.

**Clarity** means that the listener can follow the events without confusion. Who did what, and to whom? What happened first, and what happened next? A narrative that requires the listener to stop and ask "Wait, who is speaking now?" has failed the test of clarity.

**Brevity** means that the narrative includes only the details that matter. Every element should serve the point; nothing should be included merely for color or completeness. Rabbinic narratives excel at this. Consider how economically the Hillel narrative conveys its information: person, time, place, weather, action, consequence — all in two sentences. There is no description of the study house's architecture, no account of what Shemayah and Avtalyon were teaching, no detailed weather report. The narrative includes exactly what is needed and nothing more.

**Plausibility** means that the events described are believable — either as things that actually happened or as things that could plausibly happen. Even when rabbinic narratives include miraculous elements (fire from heaven, trees singing), these miracles serve a theological point and operate within the narrative logic of the story. They are not random; they are meaningful.

Think of these three virtues as the quality-control checklist for your own narrative compositions. After writing a ma'aseh, ask yourself:
- Is it *clear*? Can the listener follow it without confusion?
- Is it *brief*? Have I included only the details that serve the point?
- Is it *plausible*? Do the events make sense within the world of the story?

### The Formulae of Rabbinic Narrative

Rabbinic narratives use distinctive opening and closing formulas that signal the listener to shift into narrative-reception mode. Learning to recognize these formulas will sharpen your reading of rabbinic texts.

**Opening formulas:**
- <span dir="rtl">מעשה ב...</span> — "It happened with..." (the most common formula)
- <span dir="rtl">פעם אחת</span> — "Once..." (introducing a single event)
- <span dir="rtl">שוב מעשה</span> — "Again, it happened..." (introducing a second narrative on the same theme)
- <span dir="rtl">כבר היה</span> — "It once was..." (introducing a past event, often with the sense "there is precedent")

**Closing formulas:**
- <span dir="rtl">באותה שעה אמרו</span> — "At that time they said..." (transitioning from narrative to ruling)
- <span dir="rtl">מכאן אמרו</span> — "From here they said..." (deriving a general principle from the narrative)
- <span dir="rtl">נמצא</span> — "It was found that..." (drawing a conclusion)

The opening formula *ma'aseh be-...* is so characteristic that it functions almost as a genre marker: when you hear it, you know that a narrative exemplum or a legal precedent is about to follow. The closing formula *mi-kan amru* ("from here they said") is equally significant: it marks the transition from narrative to normative — from "this happened" to "therefore this is the rule." This transition is the moment when the ma'aseh does its work: the specific case generates the general principle.

### The Ma'aseh and Legal Reasoning

One of the most distinctive features of rabbinic narrative is the intimate relationship between storytelling and legal reasoning. In many legal systems, cases and rules exist in separate domains: the rules are abstract and general; the cases are concrete and particular. In rabbinic law, the two are woven together so tightly that it is often impossible to understand a rule without the narrative that generated it, or a narrative without the rule it illustrates.

Consider this passage from Mishnah Sukkah 2:7:

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה והביאו לו לרבן יוחנן בן זכאי לטעום את התבשיל, ולרבן גמליאל שני כותבות ודלי של מים, ואמרו: העלום לסוכה</span>
>
> *It happened that they brought to Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai a cooked dish to taste, and to Rabban Gamaliel two dates and a bucket of water, and they said: Take them up to the sukkah.*[^50]

The narrative establishes a standard of practice: these sages, when presented with even a small amount of food, insisted on eating it in the sukkah. The ma'aseh functions as a legal exemplum: if these great sages applied the sukkah obligation even to casual snacking, then surely the law requires at least that much of everyone.

This interweaving of narrative and law is one of the most powerful rhetorical features of the Mishnah. It means that legal rules are never merely abstract — they are always connected to real people making real decisions in real situations. The student who learns the law through the narrative understands not just *what* the rule is but *why* it exists and *how* it applies. The narrative is not decoration added to the law; it is the medium through which the law becomes comprehensible.

### Exercises

**Try This:** Read the following brief narrative and identify all six elements (person, action, time, place, manner, cause). Then ask: which elements does the narrative include, which does it omit, and why?

> <span dir="rtl">מעשה ברבי אליעזר שהיה יושב ודורש כל היום כולו בהלכות החג. יצתה כת ראשונה... יצתה כת שנייה... יצתה כת שלישית</span>
>
> *It happened with Rabbi Eliezer that he was sitting and teaching the laws of the festival all day long. The first group departed... the second group departed... the third group departed...*

**Your Turn:** Compose a ma'aseh — a brief narrative in the rabbinic style — that illustrates one of the following principles:
- "Greater is the service of Torah scholars than its study" (one who serves learns more than one who merely studies)
- "Do not judge your fellow until you have reached his place" (you cannot understand someone's choices until you have been in their situation)

Your narrative should:
1. Begin with an appropriate formula (*ma'aseh be-...* or *pa'am achat*)
2. Include at least four of the six narrative elements
3. End with a closing formula that derives a principle from the narrative
4. Be brief enough to read aloud in under one minute

## 2.4. Chreia: The Wise Saying Attributed to a Person

### What Is a Chreia?

Of all the building blocks of rhetoric, the chreia stands at the center. The Greek rhetoricians defined it precisely: **a concise statement or action, attributed with aptness to a specific, named person.**[^51] Three qualities are essential:

1. **Conciseness** — it is brief, not an extended discourse
2. **Attribution** — it is ascribed to a specific, named individual
3. **Aptness** — the statement or action fits the character to whom it is attributed

Pirkei Avot — the most widely known collection of rabbinic wisdom — is, in rhetorical terms, a chreia anthology. When the Mishnah says "Hillel says..." and then records a concise, pointed teaching, that is a chreia. The entire structure of Avot — sage by sage, each with his signature utterance, arranged in chains of pedagogical transmission — is the structure of a chreia collection. And it is one of the greatest such collections in all of ancient literature. Nothing comparable in scope or systematic organization survives from the Greek or Roman world.[^52]

Why does the chreia matter? Because it is the form in which a sage's authority is most directly transmitted. A mashal can be told by anyone — it floats free of its author. A ma'aseh can be narrated about anyone — the narrator matters less than the events. But a chreia is inseparable from the sage who uttered it. When we say "Hillel says: If I am not for myself, who is for me?" the force of the teaching depends *in part* on the fact that *Hillel* said it — Hillel, the paradigm of patience and humility. The same words attributed to a lesser figure would carry less weight.

This is why the tradition insists on proper attribution:

> <span dir="rtl">כל האומר דבר בשם אומרו מביא גאולה לעולם</span>
>
> *Whoever says a thing in the name of the one who said it brings redemption to the world.*[^53]

Attribution is not merely an ethical obligation — it is a rhetorical one. The chreia's power comes from the union of the saying with the sayer. Strip away the name, and you have a maxim — still valuable, but lacking the personal authority that gives the chreia its edge.

### The Difference Between Chreia, Ma'aseh, Mashal, and Klal

Let us sharpen the distinctions among our four building blocks, because clarity here will save you confusion later:

| Form | Fictional or Factual? | Attributed? | Brief? | Primary Function |
|------|----------------------|-------------|--------|------------------|
| Mashal | Fictional | Optional | Yes | Illustrate a truth |
| Ma'aseh | Factual (claimed) | Yes (named people) | Variable | Establish precedent |
| Chreia | Factual (claimed) | Yes (named sage) | Always brief | Transmit authority |
| Klal | Neither | No | Usually brief | State a general rule |

The chreia is distinct from the mashal because it claims to be factual. It is distinct from the ma'aseh because it is always concise — a single utterance or a single act, captured in a sentence or two, never an extended narrative. And it is distinct from the klal (maxim) because it is always attributed to a named person.

These distinctions are not merely academic. They affect how the form functions in argument. A mashal cannot serve as legal precedent; a chreia can. A klal carries the anonymous authority of collective wisdom; a chreia carries the specific authority of a named sage. A ma'aseh may include details irrelevant to the teaching; a chreia strips everything down to the essential point.

### Types of Chreiai

The Greek rhetorician Theon classified chreiai into three main types, with further subdivisions. The rabbinic tradition contains rich examples of every type. Learning this taxonomy will help you both recognize chreiai in your reading and compose them in your own work.[^54]

#### A. Sayings-Chreiai

These make their point in words — a sage says something concise and pointed. Within this category, there are two main species:

**1. Unprompted Statements — The sage simply declares something.**

This is the overwhelmingly dominant type in Pirkei Avot. The formula is "Rabbi X says" (<span dir="rtl">רבי פלוני אומר</span>) or "He used to say" (<span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר</span>):

> <span dir="rtl">שמעון הצדיק היה משירי כנסת הגדולה. הוא היה אומר: על שלושה דברים העולם עומד — על התורה ועל העבודה ועל גמילות חסדים</span>
>
> *Simeon the Righteous was among the last of the Great Assembly. He used to say: On three things the world stands — on Torah, on worship, and on deeds of lovingkindness.* (Avot 1:2)[^55]

> <span dir="rtl">הוא היה אומר: אם אין אני לי, מי לי? וכשאני לעצמי, מה אני? ואם לא עכשיו, אימתי?</span>
>
> *He [Hillel] used to say: If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am only for myself, what am I? And if not now, when?* (Avot 1:14)[^56]

> <span dir="rtl">רבי טרפון אומר: היום קצר, והמלאכה מרובה, והפועלים עצלים, והשכר הרבה, ובעל הבית דוחק</span>
>
> *Rabbi Tarfon says: The day is short, the work is great, the workers are lazy, the reward is great, and the Master of the house is pressing.* (Avot 2:15)[^57]

Notice the formula *hu hayah omer* — "he used to say." This indicates a habitual saying — something the sage repeated on multiple occasions, a teaching so characteristic that it became his signature. The Greek chreia collections use a similar formula: *ephe* ("he used to say"). The parallel is striking.

Note also the distinction between *omer* (present tense: "says") and *hayah omer* (past habitual: "used to say"). The present tense suggests a teaching that is timeless and still operative — "Rabbi X says" implies his teaching stands as wisdom for all time. The past habitual suggests a teaching that characterized the sage during his lifetime — it is retrospective and biographical. Paying attention to these formulaic distinctions enriches your understanding of how the tradition frames each teaching.

**Try This:** Take Hillel's chreia — "If I am not for myself, who is for me? And when I am only for myself, what am I? And if not now, when?" — and analyze its rhetorical structure. How many questions does it pose? What is the relationship between them? Does each question build on the previous one? What is the effect of ending with three unanswered questions rather than three declarative statements? We will return to this chreia repeatedly throughout the book, analyzing it through different lenses. For now, simply notice its structure.

**2. Circumstantial Statements — The sage reacts to something observed or experienced.**

These are more dramatic than unprompted statements because they have a setting and a trigger. The formula often includes "he saw" (<span dir="rtl">ראה</span>) or "when he saw" (<span dir="rtl">כשראה</span>):

> <span dir="rtl">אף הוא ראה גולגולת אחת שצפה על פני המים. אמר לה: על דאטפת אטפוך, וסוף מטיפייך יטופון</span>
>
> *He [Hillel] also saw a skull floating on the water. He said to it: Because you drowned others, they drowned you; and in the end, those who drowned you will themselves be drowned.* (Avot 2:7)[^58]

Study this chreia carefully — it is a work of extraordinary craft. The *circumstance* (a skull floating on water) is vivid and haunting. The *remark* is structured in Aramaic with rhythmic alliteration based on the root *t-f* (float/drown): *al de-ateft atfukh, ve-sof metifayikh yitufun*. The sound-pattern is percussive, almost incantatory — untranslatable but devastating in the original. And the *moral* operates on three levels simultaneously: the immediate (this skull was a murderer who was murdered), the intermediate (retributive justice is real), and the cosmic (the cycle of violence is self-perpetuating). Three levels of meaning compressed into a single sentence — the rabbinic ideal of maximum meaning in minimum words.

Compare this with another circumstantial chreia:

> <span dir="rtl">כשבא רבי שמעון בן יוחי מן המערה, ראה אדם אחד חורש וזורע. אמר: מניחין חיי עולם ועוסקין בחיי שעה?</span>
>
> *When Rabbi Shimon bar Yohai came out from the cave, he saw a man plowing and sowing. He said: They abandon eternal life and busy themselves with temporal life?*[^59]

The circumstance gives the saying its edge. Rabbi Shimon had spent years hiding in a cave, sustained by a miraculous spring and a carob tree, devoting every waking moment to Torah study. When he emerges and sees ordinary people engaged in ordinary agriculture, his reaction is one of shock — how can they waste their time on *this*? The saying would be merely pious without the cave; with the cave behind it, it becomes a clash of worldviews, a collision between the absolute and the everyday.

**3. Responses — The sage is asked a question and answers it.**

This is where the chreia becomes most interactive and often most memorable:

*Response to a simple question:*

> <span dir="rtl">שוב מעשה בנכרי אחד שבא לפני הלל. אמר לו: גיירני על מנת שתלמדני כל התורה כולה כשאני עומד על רגל אחת. אמר לו: דעלך סני לחברך לא תעביד — זו היא כל התורה כולה, ואידך פירושה הוא, זיל גמור</span>
>
> *A gentile came before Hillel. He said: Convert me on the condition that you teach me the entire Torah while I stand on one foot. He said to him: That which is hateful to you, do not do to your neighbor — this is the entire Torah, and the rest is commentary. Go and learn.*[^60]

The inquiry is extreme — compress all of Torah into one sentence, deliverable in the time a man can balance on one foot. The response is brilliant: a single ethical principle is offered as the essence, with everything else designated as "commentary." And the final imperative — "Go and learn" — adds an essential dimension: the answer is sufficient as a summary but insufficient as an education. The chreia opens rather than closes the dialogue of learning.

Think of the rhetorical skill here. Hillel could have responded with anger (as Shammai famously did to the same questioner). He could have said the question was absurd. Instead, he took the impossible challenge at face value and met it — brilliantly — with a response that simultaneously answers the question, rebukes the questioner's presumption (you think Torah can be learned in a moment? Go and learn!), and invites genuine engagement with the tradition. That is rhetorical mastery.

*Response to an inquiry (requiring a longer answer):*

The most celebrated example is Ben Zoma's fourfold redefinition:

> <span dir="rtl">בן זומא אומר: איזהו חכם? הלומד מכל אדם, שנאמר: מכל מלמדי השכלתי. איזהו גיבור? הכובש את יצרו, שנאמר: טוב ארך אפים מגיבור. איזהו עשיר? השמח בחלקו, שנאמר: יגיע כפיך כי תאכל, אשריך וטוב לך. איזהו מכובד? המכבד את הבריות, שנאמר: כי מכבדי אכבד</span>
>
> *Ben Zoma says: Who is wise? One who learns from every person, as it says: "From all my teachers I grew wise." Who is mighty? One who conquers his inclination, as it says: "Better is the patient one than the mighty." Who is rich? One who is happy with his portion, as it says: "When you eat the labor of your hands, happy are you." Who is honored? One who honors other people, as it says: "For those who honor Me I will honor."* (Avot 4:1)[^61]

Each of the four inquiries follows the same rhetorical pattern: a conventional term is proposed (wise, mighty, rich, honored), redefined in counter-intuitive fashion, and confirmed with a scriptural proof-text. The chreia layers multiple rhetorical forms — inquiry-response, paradoxical maxim, and scriptural confirmation — within a single compact unit. The technique of *redefinition* — taking a familiar word and giving it a surprising new meaning — is one of the most powerful moves in the rhetorical repertoire. We will return to Ben Zoma's chreia when we study invention (Part III, where we will analyze how the arguments work) and comparison (Part VII, where we will analyze how the contrasts function).

*Response to a remark or provocation:*

> <span dir="rtl">אמרו לו: עקביא, חזור בך בארבעה דברים שהיית אומר ונעשך אב בית דין לישראל. אמר להם: מוטב לי להיקרא שוטה כל ימי ולא להיעשות שעה אחת רשע לפני המקום</span>
>
> *They said to him: Akavya, retract the four things you used to say and we will make you head of the court of Israel. He said to them: Better for me to be called a fool all my days than to become wicked even for one hour before God.*[^62]

The remark is an offer — retract your opinions for political advancement. The response is a refusal so principled that it has become a paradigm of intellectual integrity. Notice the stakes: the offer is attractive (head of the court!), the sacrifice is real (being called a fool for one's entire life), and yet the sage chooses integrity over advancement. The chreia works because the tension between the offer and the refusal is maximal.

#### B. Action-Chreiai

Not every chreia is a saying. Sometimes the sage makes his point through an action — without speech, or with speech that is secondary to the act.

> <span dir="rtl">גער בו ודחפו באמת הבניין שבידו</span>
>
> *He [Shammai] rebuked him and drove him out with the builder's cubit in his hand.*[^63]

Shammai's action — physically ejecting a would-be convert with the measuring rod he happened to be holding — makes a point that no verbal response could match. And the detail of the builder's cubit (Shammai was a builder by trade) anchors the action in character: the tool of his craft becomes the instrument of his rejection.

The most powerful action-chreia in the entire tradition:

> <span dir="rtl">בשעה שהוציאו את רבי עקיבא להריגה, זמן קריאת שמע היה, והיו סורקין את בשרו במסרקות של ברזל, והיה מקבל עליו עול מלכות שמים</span>
>
> *At the time when they took Rabbi Akiva out to be executed, it was the time for reciting the Shema, and they were combing his flesh with iron combs, and he was accepting upon himself the yoke of the kingdom of Heaven.*[^64]

What Rabbi Akiva *experienced* — torture and execution — became the supreme demonstration of the commandment to love God "with all your soul" — even, as the sages interpreted it, "even if He takes your soul." The passive suffering *is* the teaching. No verbal chreia could convey the same depth of commitment.

#### C. Mixed Chreiai

Some chreiai combine speech and action, with each reinforcing the other:

> <span dir="rtl">עמד רבן גמליאל ושימש</span>
>
> *Rabban Gamaliel stood and served [the food].*[^65]

The patriarch — the highest-ranking sage — rises from his seat at a banquet and personally serves food to the other guests. When his students protest that this is beneath his dignity, he responds: "Abraham served his guests, and he was greater than all of us." The action (serving) and the speech (citing Abraham as precedent) work together to make the point: true greatness lies in service, not in being served.

#### D. Double Chreiai

The *machloqet* — the formal dispute between two sages — is the rabbinic tradition's most characteristic form of double chreia:

> <span dir="rtl">בית שמאי אומרים: בערב כל אדם יטו ויקראו, ובבוקר יעמדו. ובית הלל אומרים: כל אדם קורין כדרכן</span>
>
> *Beit Shammai say: In the evening everyone should recline and recite [the Shema], and in the morning they should stand. Beit Hillel say: Everyone recites in their own way.* (Mishnah Berakhot 1:3)[^66]

Two schools, opposing positions, each with scriptural support. Each position is an independent chreia; together they form a double chreia that frames the debate and invites the student to consider both sides. The tradition preserves both positions even after ruling in favor of Beit Hillel, because — as we learned in Part I — "both these and these are the words of the living God."

### The Eight Exercises with Chreiai

The Greek rhetorician Theon describes eight exercises to be performed with chreiai, each training a different skill. All eight are reflected in the practices of the bet midrash. These exercises are the heart of chreia-based training, and you should practice each one.[^67]

**1. Recitation** — Reporting the chreia accurately, in the same words or in other words.

The phenomenon of *variant recitation* is pervasive in rabbinic literature. Hillel's Golden Rule appears in several forms:

- Aramaic negative: "That which is hateful to you, do not do to your neighbor" (Shabbat 31a)
- Hebrew positive: "Love your neighbor as yourself" (attributed to Rabbi Akiva as a great principle, Sifra Qedoshim)
- The "three pillars" saying appears in two versions with different verbs: "The world *stands*" (Avot 1:2) vs. "The world *endures*" (Avot 1:18)

Each variant is a recitation exercise — the same teaching restated in different words. The student who can only repeat a teaching in its original words has *memorized* but not *understood* it. The student who can restate it in her own words — preserving the substance while varying the expression — has demonstrated genuine comprehension.

**Try This:** Take the chreia "The day is short, the work is great, the workers are lazy, the reward is great, and the Master of the house is pressing" (Rabbi Tarfon, Avot 2:15). Restate it in three different ways: once in plain modern language, once as an extended metaphor drawn from a different domain (not the workplace), and once in the most compressed form you can manage — three words or fewer, if possible.

**2. Inflection** — Changing the variables in a chreia to produce all possible combinations.

Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai poses the same inquiry twice: "What is the right path?" (Avot 2:9) and "What is the evil path?" (Avot 2:11). The students' answers are systematically inflected: "good eye" becomes "evil eye," "good companion" becomes "evil companion." This is the inflection exercise in action.

The fourfold permutation schemes of Avot chapter 5 are even more systematic: two binary variables crossed to produce four logical outcomes:

> <span dir="rtl">ארבע מידות בדעות: נוח לכעוס ונוח לרצות — יצא שכרו בהפסדו. קשה לכעוס וקשה לרצות — יצא הפסדו בשכרו. קשה לכעוס ונוח לרצות — חסיד. נוח לכעוס וקשה לרצות — רשע</span>
>
> *Four types of temperament: Easy to anger and easy to appease — the gain is offset by the loss. Hard to anger and hard to appease — the loss is offset by the gain. Hard to anger and easy to appease — pious. Easy to anger and hard to appease — wicked.* (Avot 5:11)[^68]

The inflection is mathematical: two variables, four combinations, each with a moral evaluation. This exercise trains systematic thinking — the ability to see all possible cases within a given category.

**3. Comment** — Evaluating a chreia as true, noble, useful, or the opposite.

> <span dir="rtl">רואה אני את דברי אלעזר בן ערך מדבריכם, שבכלל דבריו דבריכם</span>
>
> *I prefer the words of Elazar ben Arakh over yours, because his words include all of yours.* (Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai, Avot 2:9)[^69]

The master does not simply declare one answer correct — he provides a *reason* for his preference (the argument of inclusion). This teaches students not just *which* chreia to prefer but *how to evaluate* competing chreiai. The comment exercise trains evaluative judgment.

**4. Objection** — Arguing against a chreia from the opposite point of view.

> <span dir="rtl">רבי עקיבא ורבי טרפון אומרים: אילו היינו בסנהדרין לא נהרג אדם מעולם. רבן שמעון בן גמליאל אומר: אף הם מרבין שופכי דמים בישראל</span>
>
> *Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Tarfon say: Had we been in the Sanhedrin, no one would ever have been executed. Rabban Shimon ben Gamaliel says: They too would thereby increase bloodshed in Israel.* (Mishnah Makkot 1:10)[^70]

The objection does not challenge the *nobility* of the chreia but its *consequences*. Regardless of good intentions, abolishing capital punishment might embolden murderers. This teaches a crucial distinction: a teaching can be morally admirable and practically dangerous at the same time. The objection exercise forces you to separate the moral quality of an argument from its practical suitability.

**5. Expansion** — Enlarging upon a chreia with stories, examples, and commentary.

The entire work *Avot de-Rabbi Natan* is a systematic expansion exercise performed on the chreiai of Pirkei Avot. Each concise saying is expanded into elaborate narratives, multiple illustrations, and extended commentary. Avot 1:12 — Hillel's "Be of the disciples of Aaron" — becomes an extended narrative cycle demonstrating how Aaron would make peace between quarreling parties, complete with dialogue, scene-setting, and multiple episodes.

**6. Condensation** — Reducing a chreia to its most essential form.

> <span dir="rtl">לפום צערא אגרא</span>
>
> *According to the effort is the reward.* (Ben He He, Avot 5:23)

An entire theology of effort and reward in three Aramaic words. This is maximum condensation — the limit of compression.

The progressive condensation described by Rabbi Simlai (Makkot 23b-24a) — from 613 commandments to 11 (Psalm 15) to 6 (Isaiah 33) to 3 (Micah 6:8) to 1 (Habakkuk 2:4: "The righteous shall live by his faith") — is a chreia *about* condensation. It performs the exercise it describes, moving from the maximally expanded form to the maximally condensed one. To condense is not merely to shorten — it is to penetrate to the essence.

**7. Refutation** — Arguing that a chreia is false, unclear, impractical, or harmful.

We will develop this skill fully in Part IV, but you should know now that the Talmud's entire dialectical method — proposition, objection, resolution — is, at its core, a series of chreia refutation exercises. Every *kushya* (difficulty) that the Talmud raises against a teaching is a refutation exercise; every *teyuvta* (definitive refutation) is the exercise carried to its conclusion.

**8. Confirmation** — Providing arguments that support and validate a chreia.

The entire structure of halakhic midrash — the Mekhilta on Exodus, the Sifra on Leviticus, the Sifrei on Numbers and Deuteronomy — is a confirmation exercise: a legal principle is stated, then supported through scriptural citation, logical inference, historical precedent, and appeal to authority. We will develop this skill fully in Part IV as well.

### Composition: Crafting Your Own Chreia

The chreia is a highly constrained form. Your composition must satisfy three conditions simultaneously:

1. **It must be concise.** If it takes more than two sentences, it is not a chreia — it is a discourse.
2. **It must be attributed.** Specify who said or did this. The attribution should be to a real, named person.
3. **It must be apt.** The saying or action should fit the character to whom it is attributed. A boorish remark does not make a chreia when attributed to a sage known for gentleness.

Beyond these three requirements, the best chreiai have a fourth quality: **pointedness.** They surprise, challenge, or reframe something familiar. A platitude is not a chreia, no matter how well attributed or briefly stated. The chreia should make the reader stop and think.

**Your Turn:**

1. **Transform by recitation.** Take Ben Zoma's "Who is wise? One who learns from every person" and restate it in three different ways — once as a command, once as a narrative, and once as a metaphor.

2. **Compose an original chreia.** Think of a person you admire — a teacher, a mentor, a public figure. Compose a chreia that captures something essential about their character: a brief saying or action, attributed to them, that reveals who they are.

3. **Expand, then condense.** Take the chreia "In a place where there are no men, strive to be a man" (Hillel, Avot 2:6). First *expand* it: write a paragraph of commentary explaining what it means and why it matters. Then *condense* it: reduce it to the shortest possible form that still captures the essential meaning. Which version is more powerful — the expanded or the condensed?

## 2.5. Klal: The Maxim That Stands Alone

### What Is a Maxim?

The maxim — *gnome* in Greek, *klal* or *divrei musar* in Hebrew — is the simplest of all rhetorical forms: **a general statement about human conduct, stated without personal attribution.** It does not tell a story (like the mashal). It does not report an event (like the ma'aseh). It does not claim the authority of a named sage (like the chreia). It simply declares a truth and lets the truth speak for itself.[^71]

Aristotle defined it precisely: a maxim is "a statement, not about a particular thing — as, for instance, what sort of person Iphicrates is — but of a general kind; and not about all things — as, for instance, that the straight is the opposite of the curved — but about all such things as are the objects of practical action."[^72] In other words: a maxim is about how to live, stated in general terms, without reference to any specific individual.

Why does the maxim matter when we already have the chreia? Because the maxim and the chreia have different sources of authority. The chreia borrows its authority from the reputation of the named speaker — you trust Hillel's teaching partly because Hillel said it. The maxim claims authority from the self-evidence of its content — you accept it because it rings true, regardless of who said it. These are two fundamentally different modes of persuasion, and the skilled speaker needs both.

There is a parallel in rabbinic law. The Mishnah distinguishes between *attributed* rulings (signed by a named sage) and *unattributed* rulings (*stam mishnah*). The unattributed ruling carries special authority precisely because it is anonymous — it represents the consensus of the sages rather than the opinion of any individual, and it is therefore harder to challenge. You can set one sage's opinion against another's, but you cannot set an individual opinion against the anonymous consensus of the tradition.[^73]

The maxim works the same way. An attributed saying invites the response "But Rabbi X disagrees!" An unattributed principle resists such challenge — there is no individual authority to dispute. The skilled speaker deploys the chreia form when the prestige of the named sage serves his argument, and the maxim form when he wants to speak with the impersonal authority of received wisdom.

### Types of Maxims

The classical rhetoricians classified maxims into four types, and the rabbinic tradition provides beautiful examples of each:[^74]

**1. The Declarative Maxim** — states what is the case, without commanding action:

> <span dir="rtl">הכל צפוי, והרשות נתונה</span>
>
> *All is foreseen, yet freedom of choice is given.* (Avot 3:15)[^75]

Two clauses, six Hebrew words, and they encompass a philosophical problem that the Stoics and Epicureans debated across centuries of elaborate treatises. Divine omniscience and human free will — the entire paradox in a single breath. The declarative maxim does not tell you what to do; it tells you what is. Its authority lies in the calm self-evidence of its statement: *this is how the world works.*

**2. The Hortatory Maxim** — commands or exhorts:

> <span dir="rtl">אל תסתכל בקנקן אלא במה שיש בו</span>
>
> *Do not look at the jar but at what is in it.* (Avot 4:20)[^76]

The imperative "do not look" establishes the tone of direct exhortation. The metaphor is drawn from the wine trade: a humble jar may hold aged vintage; a splendid amphora may hold vinegar. The maxim commands a specific mode of judgment: look past appearances to substance. "Don't judge a book by its cover" is the modern equivalent — but the rabbinic version is more vivid, more concrete, and more memorable.

**3. The Compound Maxim** — states a principle and its reason:

> <span dir="rtl">חנוך לנער על פי דרכו, גם כי יזקין לא יסור ממנה</span>
>
> *Train a child according to his way; even when he grows old he will not depart from it.* (Proverbs 22:6)

The first clause states the precept: educate the child according to his individual nature. The second clause provides the justification: habits formed in youth persist. This is the maxim *with reasons* — more persuasive than the bare imperative because it shows *why* the command makes sense.

**4. The Double Maxim** — presents thesis and antithesis in balanced structure:

> <span dir="rtl">אם אין קמח אין תורה, אם אין תורה אין קמח</span>
>
> *If there is no flour, there is no Torah; if there is no Torah, there is no flour.* (Avot 3:17)[^77]

This may be the most perfectly constructed maxim in all of rabbinic literature. Two conditional clauses, each the mirror image of the other, together expressing a relationship of mutual dependence that neither clause alone could capture. Material sustenance requires spiritual purpose; spiritual pursuit requires material sustenance. Were either clause stated alone, the result would be a partisan argument (either for practical life or for contemplative life). Stated together, they constitute a comprehensive philosophical position: body and spirit are mutually sustaining. The symmetrical structure *is* the meaning.

### The Transformation Between Chreia and Maxim

Here is something important that the student of rhetoric must understand: **chreiai and maxims convert into each other.** A chreia becomes a maxim when the attribution is dropped — when a sage's teaching becomes so thoroughly absorbed into common wisdom that the community forgets (or no longer cares) who said it first. A maxim becomes a chreia when the tradition reattaches it to a named sage, lending it the additional authority of personal reputation.

This transformation has enormous rhetorical significance. By adding or removing attribution, you can alter the *character* of a teaching without changing its *content*:

- "Rabbi Tarfon says: It is not your obligation to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it." → Chreia. Can be challenged: "But Rabbi X might disagree."
- "It is not your obligation to complete the work, but neither are you free to desist from it." → Maxim. Harder to challenge: it speaks with the anonymous authority of received wisdom.

The skilled speaker knows when to deploy each form. Use the chreia when the prestige of the named sage strengthens your argument. Use the maxim when you want your teaching to sound like universal truth rather than one person's opinion.

**Try This:** The teaching "According to the effort is the reward" (<span dir="rtl">לפום צערא אגרא</span>) is attributed to Ben He He in Avot 5:23 — a chreia. But it also circulates widely as an anonymous proverb. Try stating it both ways — with attribution and without — and notice how the feel changes. With the attribution, it is a specific sage's insight. Without it, it is a fact about the universe.

### The Paradoxical Maxim

The most powerful maxims are the ones that challenge received opinion — that take a familiar concept and redefine it in a surprising way. Ben Zoma's fourfold redefinition (Avot 4:1) is the supreme example:

- Wisdom is redefined as teachability rather than erudition
- Might is redefined as self-mastery rather than martial prowess
- Wealth is redefined as contentment rather than abundance
- Honor is redefined as giving respect rather than receiving it

Each redefinition overturns the popular understanding and replaces it with a moral one. Aristotle observed that paradoxical maxims are especially effective because "they attract attention because of the novelty of the statement."[^78] The listener expects one thing and gets another — and that moment of surprise is when learning happens.

The paradoxical maxim is a tool you will use throughout your rhetorical career. Whenever you want to challenge your audience's assumptions, take a familiar term and redefine it. The technique works because it preserves the vocabulary of the conventional understanding (everyone knows what "wisdom" means) while replacing its content (but wisdom is not what you thought it was).

### Exercises

**Try This:** Classify the following as chreia or maxim. For each maxim, identify its type (declarative, hortatory, compound, or double):

1. "Be deliberate in judgment, raise up many disciples, and make a fence around the Torah" (Avot 1:1)
2. "Love work, hate mastery, and do not make yourself known to the government" (Shemayah, Avot 1:10)
3. "The world endures on three things: on justice, on truth, and on peace" (Avot 1:18)

**Your Turn:**

1. **Compose original maxims.** Write one maxim of each type (declarative, hortatory, compound, double) on the theme of *speech and silence*. Each should be concise enough to memorize in a single reading.

2. **Transform chreia to maxim and back.** Take Hillel's "If I am not for myself, who is for me?" and strip it to a maxim — state the same truth without attribution and without rhetorical questions. Then take the anonymous maxim "A person does not sin unless a spirit of folly enters him" and compose a narrative frame that transforms it into a chreia: who said it, under what circumstances, and to whom?

3. **Analyze the Avot chain.** Read through Pirkei Avot 1:1-1:18 and classify every teaching as chreia or maxim. For each chreia, ask: could this teaching stand as a maxim if the attribution were removed? Would it gain or lose force?

## 2.6. Part II Challenges

These exercises integrate everything you have learned in Part II. Each one requires you to combine skills from multiple sections.

**Challenge 1: The Sage's Teaching Session.** Choose a single teaching from Pirkei Avot. Compose the following:
- A *mashal* (parable) that illustrates the teaching, complete with nimshal
- A *ma'aseh* (narrative) that dramatizes the teaching being applied in a real situation
- An analysis of the teaching as a *chreia* — identifying its type, its rhetorical structure, and what makes it apt to its attributed speaker
- A transformation of the teaching into a *klal* (maxim), with a brief comment on what is gained and lost by removing the attribution

**Challenge 2: Chreia Elaboration.** Take one of these chreiai and perform all eight exercises from the chreia training:
- Hillel's "If I am not for myself, who is for me?"
- Ben Zoma's "Who is wise? One who learns from every person."
- Akavya's "Better to be called a fool all my days than to become wicked for one hour."

For each chreia: (1) recite it in three different formulations, (2) inflect it by reversing or varying its terms, (3) comment on its truth and usefulness, (4) object to it from the opposite point of view, (5) expand it with a story and additional examples, (6) condense it to the fewest possible words, (7) identify a ground on which it could be refuted, (8) confirm it with evidence from another source.

**Challenge 3: Compose a Passage.** Write a short passage (300-500 words) in which all four building blocks appear: a mashal, a ma'aseh, a chreia, and a klal. The passage should have a single unifying theme, and each form should contribute to the argument in its distinctive way. Model your composition on the style of Avot de-Rabbi Natan, where chreiai are expanded with narratives, parables, and general principles woven together.

---

# Part III: FINDING YOUR ARGUMENTS — THE ART OF INVENTION

## 3.1. Where Arguments Come From

You have now mastered the four building blocks — the mashal, the ma'aseh, the chreia, and the klal. You can tell a story, compose a parable, quote a sage, and state a principle. But knowing *how* to present an argument is only half the battle. Before you can present an argument, you need to *find* one.

That is what invention is about. In the classical tradition, invention (*inventio* in Latin, *heuresis* in Greek) is the art of discovering what to say — of surveying a problem and identifying the arguments available to you. It is the first and most fundamental of the five rhetorical skills, because without it the other four have nothing to work with. You can arrange beautifully, style elegantly, remember perfectly, and deliver brilliantly — but if you have no argument to begin with, all those skills are useless.[^79]

In the bet midrash, the master question of invention is two words long:

> <span dir="rtl">מנא הני מילי?</span>
>
> *From where do you derive this?*[^80]

This question — *minayin?*, "from where?" — is the engine of rabbinic invention. It demands that every claim be traced to its source, every ruling connected to its foundation. You cannot simply assert something in the bet midrash; you must show where it comes from. And the tools for answering that question — the hermeneutical rules called *middot* — are the most systematic toolkit for finding arguments that any ancient civilization produced.

Think of the difference between an untrained and a trained mind confronting a legal question. The untrained mind says, "I think the answer is X." The trained mind says, "The answer is X, and here is where it comes from: this verse, interpreted through this rule, in conjunction with this precedent, confirmed by this analogy." The difference is not intelligence — it is *method*. Invention gives you a method for finding arguments that the unaided mind would miss.

The Greek rhetoricians organized their tools of invention into a system of *topoi* — "topics" or "places" where arguments can be found. Aristotle devoted an entire work (the *Topics*) to cataloging them. Think of topoi as a checklist: when you need an argument, you run through the list of standard types — argument from analogy, argument from cause, argument from consequence, argument from authority, argument from definition — and see which ones apply to your situation. Each topos is a "place" in the sense that it is a location where arguments are stored, waiting for the rhetorician to come and collect them.[^81]

The rabbinic tradition has its own system of topoi — the thirteen *middot* of Rabbi Ishmael — and it is more compact, more precise, and arguably more powerful than the Greek system, because the rabbinic middot are not just logical rules but keys to a sacred text. They do not merely help you find arguments; they help you find arguments *in the Torah*, unlocking meaning that the untrained reader cannot see.

## 3.2. The Thirteen Middot as an Argument-Finding System

The foundation of rabbinic inventive method is the *Baraita de-Rabbi Ishmael* — a list of thirteen hermeneutical rules recited every morning as part of the liturgy. These are the sage's primary instruments for extracting legal principles from Scripture, and they constitute a complete system for generating arguments.[^82]

We will study the most important of these rules in detail. For each one, we will explain the logical principle, give a classic example, and then show you how to apply it in your own argumentation.

### Rule 1: Qal Va-Homer (A Fortiori — "Light and Heavy")

> <span dir="rtl">קל וחומר</span>

**The principle:** If something is true in a lesser case, it must be true in a greater case. Conversely, if something is *not* true in a greater case, it cannot be true in a lesser case.

This is the most natural of all argumentative forms. Even children use it: "If Dad won't let me stay up late on a school night, he *certainly* won't let me stay up late on a night before an exam." The qal va-homer simply formalizes this intuition.

**Classic example:** A Nazirite, who has committed no sin, is nevertheless forbidden to drink wine. How much more, then, should a person who *has* sinned refrain from pleasurable indulgence? The lesser case (the innocent Nazirite) establishes the principle; the greater case (the sinner) applies it with increased force.

**The built-in constraint:** The sages imposed a crucial rule on the qal va-homer: <span dir="rtl">דיו לבא מן הדין להיות כנדון</span> — "It is sufficient for the derived case to be equal to the source case."[^83] This means you cannot use a qal va-homer to derive a *stricter* conclusion than your premise warrants. If the lesser case gets punishment X, the greater case gets *at most* punishment X — not punishment X-plus-more. This constraint prevents the argument from spiraling upward without limit. It is, in effect, a built-in check on rhetorical excess.

Think of how a modern lawyer might use this: "Your Honor, if the law prohibits even *accidental* disclosure of confidential information, surely it prohibits *intentional* disclosure." That is a qal va-homer. But the lawyer cannot then argue that intentional disclosure should be punished *more severely* than the statute provides for accidental disclosure — that would violate the *dayo* principle.

**Try This:** Construct a qal va-homer argument for the following proposition: "If even a king must stand when the Torah scroll passes by, then certainly an ordinary person must do so." Identify the lesser case, the greater case, and the principle that connects them. Then consider: what would the *dayo* constraint prevent you from concluding?

### Rule 2: Gezerah Shavah (Verbal Analogy)

> <span dir="rtl">גזירה שוה</span>

**The principle:** When the same word or phrase appears in two different legal contexts, the law of one context may be applied to the other.

This rule is immensely powerful — and therefore subject to an important restriction. Because any two passages might share a word by coincidence, the sages ruled that no one may innovate a gezerah shavah on his own authority. You must have received the verbal link as a tradition from your teacher.[^84] This restriction teaches an important lesson about invention: some argumentative tools are too powerful to be deployed without external authorization. Not every argument you *can* make is an argument you *should* make.

**Classic example:** The word <span dir="rtl">מועד</span> ("appointed time") appears in connection with both the Passover offering and the daily offering. From this verbal parallel, the sages derive that just as the daily offering overrides the Sabbath, so too does the Passover offering.

The gezerah shavah works because it treats shared vocabulary as evidence of shared legal principle. When two legal passages use the same key word, the gezerah shavah presumes that the word creates a bridge between them — that what is true of the word in one context is true of it in the other. It is, in modern terms, an argument from textual *intertextuality*.

### Rule 3: Binyan Av (General Principle from a Particular Case)

> <span dir="rtl">בנין אב</span>

**The principle:** From a single explicit case, a general rule is derived that applies to all analogous cases.

This is the rabbinic form of *induction* — moving from the particular to the universal. The sage looks at one clear case in Scripture, identifies the *principle* that makes it work, and then applies that principle to other cases that share the relevant characteristic.

**Classic example:** Scripture explicitly commands rest on the Sabbath for "your male slave and your female slave." From this single case, the sage constructs a binyan av: if even slaves, who have limited legal obligations, must rest, then the principle of Sabbath rest extends broadly. But — and this is the crucial part — the extension applies only where the cases share the relevant characteristic (*tzad ha-shaveh*). The student must learn to identify both what the cases share and where the analogy breaks down.

The binyan av is the most intellectually demanding of the middot because it requires you to identify the *essential* characteristic of a case — the feature that makes it paradigmatic — and distinguish it from the *incidental* characteristics that are merely particular to that one case. This is the same skill that a scientist uses when she extracts a general law from experimental data: she must determine which features of the experiment are essential to the result and which are merely accidental.

### Rules 5-7: Kelal u-Ferat (General and Specific)

> <span dir="rtl">כלל ופרט... פרט וכלל... כלל ופרט וכלל</span>

These three rules govern the relationship between general categories and specific instances in legal texts. They answer the question: when a text states a general principle and then gives a specific example, does the general principle apply to everything, or only to things like the specific example?

**Rule 5 — General followed by specific:** The law applies only to the specific case. The general statement is limited by the particular instance that follows.

**Rule 6 — Specific followed by general:** The law applies to all cases covered by the general category. The specific case merely introduces the topic; the general statement expands the scope.

**Rule 7 — General, then specific, then general again:** The law applies to cases that are *similar to* the specific case — not limited to the specific case alone, but not extended to everything under the sun either. The specific case serves as a paradigm, and the law extends to cases that share its essential characteristics.

This last rule — kelal u-ferat u-khelal — is one of the most elegant in the system. It creates a middle ground between absolute specificity (rule 5) and absolute generality (rule 6). The student must ask: "What is the essential characteristic of the specific example? What other cases share that characteristic?" This is the same analytical skill you use when a legal precedent is set by one case and you must determine what other cases it applies to.

### Rule 13: Two Verses That Contradict Each Other

> <span dir="rtl">שני כתובים המכחישים זה את זה — עד שיבוא הכתוב השלישי ויכריע ביניהם</span>
>
> *Two verses that contradict each other — the contradiction stands until a third verse comes and resolves between them.*[^85]

This final rule addresses apparent contradictions — and it teaches a profound principle: **contradictions are not failures; they are invitations to deeper understanding.** When two sources seem to say opposite things, the sage does not reject either one. Instead, she searches for a resolution that shows both to be true in their proper spheres.

**Classic example:** One verse says God "came down upon Mount Sinai" (Exodus 19:20); another says God "spoke from the heavens" (Deuteronomy 4:36). Did God descend to earth or remain in heaven? A third verse resolves the contradiction: "From heaven He made you hear His voice... and on earth He showed you His great fire" (Deuteronomy 4:36) — the divine voice came from heaven, but its effects were manifested on earth.

This technique is immensely useful beyond the study of sacred texts. Whenever you encounter two apparently contradictory pieces of evidence, resist the temptation to discard one of them. Instead, search for the framework that accommodates both. The contradiction is usually a sign that you have not yet understood the full picture — that each piece of evidence is true in a domain you have not yet identified. This is one of the most valuable habits of mind that the study of rhetoric can develop.

**Your Turn:** You are presented with two conflicting rulings:
- "A person should always teach his student in concise language" (the virtue of brevity)
- "A person should expound from multiple angles until the student understands" (the virtue of thoroughness)

These seem contradictory. Using Rule 13, find the resolution: under what circumstances does each rule apply? What "third verse" — what mediating principle — reconciles them?

## 3.3. Stasis Theory: The Four Questions Every Dispute Answers

The middot tell you *where* to find arguments in a text. But what if the question is not "What does this text mean?" but "What is the right answer to this dispute?" For that, you need a different tool: *stasis theory*.

Stasis theory comes from the Greek rhetorical tradition, where it was developed by Hermagoras of Temnos (second century BCE) and refined by Cicero and Quintilian. The core insight is simple but transformative: **every dispute, no matter how complex, ultimately turns on one of four questions.** If you can identify which question your dispute is really about, you have already won half the argument — because you know where to direct your energy.[^86]

The four questions are:

### Question 1: Conjecture — Did It Happen?

> <span dir="rtl">האם היה? האם קרה?</span>

This is the question of fact. Did the event occur? Did the person perform the act? In modern terms: "Is the factual premise true?" In the bet din, this is the realm of witness examination — the *derishah* (probing inquiry) and *haqirah* (detailed cross-examination) by which the judges test whether the events described by the witnesses actually occurred.[^87]

The Mishnah specifies seven fixed inquiries that every witness must answer:

> <span dir="rtl">שבע חקירות: באיזה שבוע? באיזו שנה? באיזה חודש? בכמה בחודש? באיזה יום? באיזו שעה? באיזה מקום?</span>
>
> *Seven inquiries: In which Sabbatical cycle? In which year? In which month? On which day of the month? On which day? At which hour? In which place?*[^88]

If the witnesses contradict each other on these fundamental questions, their testimony is invalidated. These seven questions are the stasis of conjecture applied to judicial proceedings — establishing the factual framework of the case.

### Question 2: Definition — What Is It?

> <span dir="rtl">מה הוא? כיצד מגדירים את הדבר?</span>

Once we have established that an event occurred, we must determine what *category* it falls into. Is this act "work" on Shabbat? Is this substance "food" for purposes of a blessing? Is this transaction a "sale" or a "gift"?

The supreme example: the thirty-nine categories of labor forbidden on Shabbat (Mishnah Shabbat 7:2). Each category is a genus with numerous species. When a novel question arises — "Is carrying an umbrella on Shabbat forbidden?" — the sage must determine which, if any, of the thirty-nine categories it falls under. The facts are not in dispute (someone carried an umbrella); the question is how to *classify* the act.[^89]

Most Mishnaic disputes are, at bottom, disputes about definition. When Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel disagree about a legal standard, they usually agree on the facts — they disagree about which category the facts belong to. The student who can identify "This is a question of definition" has already clarified the dispute enormously, because she knows that the argument should focus on boundary-drawing, not fact-finding.

### Question 3: Quality — What Is Its Character?

> <span dir="rtl">כיצד הוא? מה טיבו של המעשה?</span>

Once we know what happened (conjecture) and what it is (definition), we must assess its moral and legal character. Was it intentional or accidental? Was there justification? Are there mitigating circumstances?

The fundamental distinction in rabbinic law is between *shogeg* (inadvertent) and *meizid* (intentional). The *act* is the same; the *classification* is the same; but the moral character — and therefore the legal consequence — differs based on the actor's state of mind. Between pure accident and full intention, the tradition recognizes multiple intermediate categories: compulsion (*ones*), unintentional action (*mitasek*), inadvertence (*shogeg*), and deliberate transgression (*meizid*). Each carries a different consequence.[^90]

The stasis of quality is where the most sophisticated legal arguments happen. Think of a modern criminal trial: the prosecution has proved that the defendant killed the victim (conjecture established, definition established as homicide). The entire trial now turns on quality: was it first-degree murder (intentional, premeditated), second-degree murder (intentional, not premeditated), manslaughter (reckless), or self-defense (justified)? The rabbinic system addresses exactly the same questions with exactly the same sophistication.

### Question 4: Procedure — Who Decides?

> <span dir="rtl">לפני מי? באיזה בית דין?</span>

Even after facts, definition, and quality are settled, the question of jurisdiction remains: which court has authority? What procedural rules apply? The Mishnah's system of jurisdictional levels — three judges for civil cases, twenty-three for capital cases, seventy-one for national matters — is itself a rhetorical statement: the gravity of the case determines the weight of the judicial process.[^91]

The skilled advocate identifies the stasis of the dispute at the outset and directs all arguments to that point. When the question is "Did it happen?" it is useless to argue about justification. When the question is "What is it?" it is useless to argue about jurisdiction. The sage who can say, "The dispute before us is a question of definition" — that sage has already clarified the debate and focused everyone's attention where it belongs.

**Try This:** Consider the famous Oven of Akhnai dispute (Bava Metzia 59b). Rabbi Eliezer argues that a certain oven is ritually pure; the majority disagrees. Identify which stasis this dispute occupies. Is it a question of conjecture (did something happen)? Definition (what category does this oven belong to)? Quality (does the character of the oven's construction matter)? Procedure (who has authority to decide)? The answer may surprise you — the narrative moves through *multiple* stases as the argument escalates.

## 3.4. Ethos, Pathos, Logos — Through Rabbinic Eyes

Aristotle identified three modes of persuasion: **ethos** (the credibility of the speaker), **pathos** (the emotions of the audience), and **logos** (the logical force of the argument). Every act of persuasion uses some combination of these three, and the skilled speaker knows how to deploy all of them.[^92]

The rabbinic tradition is rich in all three modes — and in certain respects, it pushes each one further than the Greek and Roman traditions did.

### Ethos: The Character of the Sage as Proof

In the rabbinic tradition, the sage's moral character is not merely helpful to his persuasive authority — it is *constitutive* of it. The ideal of <span dir="rtl">תוכו כברו</span> — "his interior is like his exterior" — is a principle of ethos: the effective speaker is one whose public speech is consistent with his private character.[^93]

When Rabban Yohanan ben Zakkai escaped the besieged Jerusalem in a coffin and addressed the Roman general Vespasian, calling him "king" before the news of his elevation had arrived, the persuasive force of his words rested primarily on his *ethos*: a man who had risked death, who spoke with prophetic authority, and whose prediction was immediately confirmed by events. The argument succeeded because the speaker had established himself — through actions, composure, and accuracy — as a person worthy of belief.[^94]

The rabbinic tradition recognizes two modes of ethos, corresponding to Quintilian's distinction between gentle ethos and commanding ethos. **Hillel** represents the gentle mode — the sage whose patience, humility, and accessibility are themselves arguments for the rightness of his positions. **Rabbi Akiva** represents the commanding mode — the sage whose personal transformation (from illiterate shepherd to greatest master of his generation) and ultimate martyrdom constitute an irrefutable testimony to the truth of his teaching.

### Pathos: The Stirring of Emotion

The rabbinic tradition deploys pathos with extraordinary skill, particularly in the narratives preserved in the aggadic literature. Consider the scene at the end of tractate Makkot, where four sages ascend to the ruins of Jerusalem:

> <span dir="rtl">כיון שהגיעו להר הבית, ראו שועל שיצא מבית קדשי הקדשים. התחילו הן בוכין ורבי עקיבא מצחק</span>
>
> *When they reached the Temple Mount, they saw a fox emerging from the Holy of Holies. They began to weep, but Rabbi Akiva laughed.*[^95]

Rabbi Akiva then explains his laughter: the prophet Uriah predicted the Temple's destruction; the prophet Zechariah predicted Jerusalem's restoration. Now that Uriah's prophecy has been fulfilled, Zechariah's must be fulfilled too. The fox — a sign of desolation for the weeping sages — becomes for Akiva a sign of future redemption.

This is a masterful deployment of what Quintilian calls *metabole* — the turning of the emotional tenor of a discourse. Rabbi Akiva transforms the entire emotional register from despair to hope through a single argumentative move that is simultaneously *logos* (a scriptural proof linking two prophecies) and *pathos* (a reversal of the audience's emotional orientation). The weeping sages respond: <span dir="rtl">עקיבא, ניחמתנו</span> — "Akiva, you have comforted us."

Notice what happened here: the *argument* (logos) produced the *emotional effect* (pathos) through the *credibility of the speaker* (ethos). All three modes of persuasion are working together, each reinforcing the others. That is the mark of truly great rhetoric.

### Logos: Proof Through Argument

Logos — proof through rational argument — is the mode to which the rabbinic tradition devotes the most sustained attention. The entire dialectical structure of the Talmud — proposition, objection, resolution, refinement — is an exercise in logos. The *qal va-homer* is an enthymematic argument. The *gezerah shavah* is analogical reasoning. The *binyan av* is induction. Every *sugya* is a sustained exercise in dialectical logic.

The characteristic form of Talmudic logical proof is:

> <span dir="rtl">יכול... תלמוד לומר</span>
>
> *One might think [X]... but the text teaches [Y].*[^96]

This formula, which pervades the tannaitic midrashim, is the rabbinic equivalent of the enthymeme: a proposition is advanced ("one might think"), a counter-proposition is derived from evidence ("the text teaches"), and the conclusion follows. The proof advances by first entertaining and then eliminating the alternative — a form of refutation embedded within confirmation.

## 3.5. Part III Challenges

**Challenge 1: The Argument Map.** Take a single passage from the Mishnah (any passage that contains a dispute between sages) and produce a complete "argument map":
- Identify the stasis of the dispute (conjecture, definition, quality, or procedure)
- Identify which middot (hermeneutical rules) each side uses to support its position
- Identify how ethos, pathos, and logos function in the arguments
- Determine which side you find more persuasive and explain *why* — using the vocabulary of invention you have learned in this Part

**Challenge 2: The Middot in Action.** Choose three of the thirteen middot and compose an original argument using each one. Your arguments should address a contemporary question (legal, ethical, or philosophical) rather than a biblical one. The point is to practice using the logical structures of the middot in your own reasoning, not merely to analyze how the sages used them.

**Challenge 3: Stasis Analysis of the Oven of Akhnai.** Read the full narrative of the Oven of Akhnai (Bava Metzia 59b). Write a detailed analysis identifying:
- Where the dispute begins (what stasis?)
- How the stasis shifts as the argument escalates (from definition to procedure to something else entirely)
- How each side deploys ethos, pathos, and logos
- Why the majority's position prevails despite Rabbi Eliezer's marshaling of miracles and a heavenly voice

This narrative is one of the most rhetorically complex passages in all of rabbinic literature. We will return to it in Part IV (for refutation), Part V (for arrangement), and Part X (for the full controversia analysis). For now, focus on the *invention* — where do the arguments come from?

---

# Part IV: BUILDING YOUR CASE — CONFIRMATION, REFUTATION, AND ARGUMENT

## 4.1. From Finding Arguments to Deploying Them

In Part III, you learned where arguments come from — the thirteen middot, the four stasis questions, the three modes of persuasion. You built an inventory of argumentative tools. Now the question changes. It is no longer "Where do I find an argument?" but "How do I use it?"

This Part introduces three of the classical progymnasmata — confirmation, refutation, and commonplace — that bridge the gap between finding arguments and building complete cases. Think of it this way: Part III gave you raw materials. Part IV teaches you to construct with them.

Confirmation is the art of proving that a proposition is true. Refutation is the art of demonstrating that a proposition fails. Commonplace is the art of amplifying what everyone already acknowledges — making the evil of a vice feel more urgent, or the beauty of a virtue shine more brightly. Together, these three exercises develop the core competence of any advocate: the ability to build up, tear down, and amplify.

We will also explore a distinctively rabbinic training ground that has no exact parallel in the Greek curriculum: the *machloqet*, the formal dispute in which two sages take opposing positions on the same question. The machloqet is where confirmation and refutation meet, where the student learns to argue both sides with equal skill and conviction.

By the end of this Part, you will be able to:
- Confirm a teaching using six distinct methods, from direct scriptural proof to practical consequence
- Refute a proposition on six distinct grounds, from ambiguity to impracticability
- Compose a commonplace speech that amplifies a virtue or condemns a vice with ascending force
- Argue both sides of a rabbinic dispute, understanding how the machloqet trains dialectical thinking
- Recognize the Mekhilta's systematic challenge-and-response structure as a masterclass in confirmation and refutation working together

Let's begin with building up before we tear down.

## 4.2. Confirmation: Proving Your Point

### What Confirmation Is

Confirmation — *kataskeue* in Greek, *qiyyum* or *re'ayah* in the language of the bet midrash — is the art of establishing that a proposition is true.[^70] It sounds simple. It is not. Anyone can assert that something is the case. The trained rhetorician demonstrates *why* it is the case, marshaling evidence from multiple sources, arranging proofs in ascending order of strength, and anticipating the objections that a skilled opponent might raise.

You have already encountered confirmation in embryonic form. Every time you grounded a chreia in Part II, you were confirming it. Every time you applied a *qal va-chomer* in Part III, you were building a confirmatory argument. Now we study confirmation as a formal discipline — learning its categories, its methods, and its distinctive rabbinic expressions.

The single most common confirmation formula in the Babylonian Talmud is one you will recognize immediately:

> <span dir="rtl">מנא הני מילי? דכתיב...</span>
>
> *From where do we know this? As it is written...*

This formula — *mina hani milli? dikhtiv...* — encapsulates the entire exercise in miniature.[^71] A proposition has been stated. The question of its ground is raised. And the ground is provided through scriptural citation. Notice what is happening structurally: the Talmud does not simply assert and move on. It pauses, asks "How do we know this?", and then provides evidence. That pause — that demand for proof — is the beating heart of confirmation as a discipline.

### The Six Methods of Confirmation

Just as refutation (which we will study next) attacks on six grounds, confirmation defends on six corresponding grounds. The two exercises are mirror images of each other. Where refutation attacks from ambiguity, confirmation defends through clarity. Where refutation attacks from improbability, confirmation supports through probability. Learning both together is essential — they are the two hands of the dialectician.[^72]

**First: From the Clear.** The strongest form of confirmation is the direct, unambiguous proof — a source that says exactly what you need it to say, requiring no interpretation, no inference, no creative reading. In the bet midrash, this means a scriptural verse whose plain sense directly supports your proposition.

The formula is beautifully simple:

> <span dir="rtl">שנאמר</span>
>
> *As it says...*

When the Talmud introduces a proof with *she-ne'emar* and quotes a verse whose meaning transparently matches the proposition, this is confirmation from the clear. Consider this example from BT Berakhot 30b:

> <span dir="rtl">מניין שאין עומדין להתפלל אלא מתוך כובד ראש? שנאמר: עבדו את ה' ביראה</span>
>
> *From where do we know that one should not stand to pray except with a solemn disposition? As it says: "Serve the Lord with reverence" (Psalm 2:11).*[^73]

The verse directly states the principle: worship requires reverence. The correspondence between proposition and proof is transparent — no interpretive labor required. This is confirmation at its most powerful and its most direct.

> **Try This:** Find three instances in the first chapter of Mishnah Berakhot where a proposition is confirmed with a direct scriptural citation. For each, assess whether the citation truly provides "clear" confirmation or whether some interpretive work is needed to connect verse to ruling.

**Second: From the Probable.** When no direct statement exists, you can confirm a proposition through logical inference — demonstrating that it follows plausibly from what is already established. This is the domain of the *qal va-chomer* and the other hermeneutical rules you studied in Part III, now deployed specifically as tools of confirmation.

The *qal va-chomer* is the premier tool of probable confirmation:

> <span dir="rtl">ומה צפרדעים שאין מצוות עליהם קידשו שם שמים, ישראל שמצוות עליהם על אחת כמה וכמה</span>
>
> *If frogs, which are not commanded, sanctified God's name by entering the ovens of Egypt, Israel, who are commanded — how much more so!*[^74]

The argument proceeds from the lesser case (frogs, who have no obligation) to the greater case (Israel, who are obligated). No verse explicitly states the conclusion, but the logical relationship between the two cases makes it compelling. This is confirmation through probability — what Aristotle calls *eikos* (the likely) and what the sages demonstrate through the elegant structure of the a fortiori argument.

Remember the *dayyo* principle you learned in Part III: the conclusion of a *qal va-chomer* cannot exceed its premise. If the lesser case carries penalty X, the greater case carries penalty X — but not X-plus-Y. This built-in restraint prevents confirmation from overreaching. The sages build checks into their most powerful confirmatory tools.

**Third: From the Possible.** Sometimes you cannot prove that a proposition is true — but you can demonstrate that no barrier prevents it from being true. This is the weakest form of confirmation, but it is strategically important because it shifts the burden of proof back to the challenger.

The Talmudic formula:

> <span dir="rtl">מי לא עסקינן דאיכא...?</span>
>
> *Are we not dealing with a case where there is...?*[^75]

This response to a challenge does not prove the proposition. It shows a plausible scenario under which the proposition holds. The effect is rhetorical judo: "You raised an objection, but I have shown a possible case where my ruling works. Now demonstrate impossibility, not merely implausibility." The burden shifts.

Think of how this works in everyday argument. If someone challenges your claim that a certain policy could work, and you show one realistic scenario where it succeeds, you have not proven your case — but you have demonstrated possibility, and now the challenger must do more than wave vaguely at difficulties.

**Fourth: From the Consistent.** A proposition gains strength when it can be shown to harmonize with other established teachings. In the Talmud, this takes the form of corroborating citations — additional sources that independently point in the same direction.

> <span dir="rtl">תניא נמי הכי</span>
>
> *A baraita also teaches thus.*

> <span dir="rtl">לימא מסייע ליה</span>
>
> *Let us say this supports him.*[^76]

Each additional supporting source strengthens the proposition. The principle is cumulative: one proof is good, two are better, three are compelling. Notice how the Talmud typically arranges these corroborating proofs in ascending order of strength, saving the most authoritative source for last. This is an arrangement principle applied to confirmation — the skilled advocate builds toward a climax.

**Fifth: From the Fitting.** A proposition is confirmed when it accords with the moral and aesthetic sensibilities of the tradition — when it feels *right* in context, when it harmonizes with the community's deepest values.

The paradigmatic example is Rabbi Akiva's famous declaration:

> <span dir="rtl">ואהבת לרעך כמוך — אמר רבי עקיבא: זה כלל גדול בתורה</span>
>
> *"Love your neighbor as yourself" (Leviticus 19:18) — Rabbi Akiva said: This is a great principle in the Torah.*[^77]

Rabbi Akiva is not confirming this through logical proof or corroborating citations. He is confirming through fittingness: a system devoted to justice and compassion *ought* to have love at its center. It is fitting that the Torah's organizing principle should be a commandment of love. The confirmation operates on moral grounds — this is what the Torah should be about, and therefore this reading is correct.

The debate with Ben Azzai in the Sifra makes this even more interesting. Ben Azzai proposes an alternative "great principle":

> <span dir="rtl">זה ספר תולדות אדם — בן עזאי אומר: זה כלל גדול מזה</span>
>
> *"This is the book of the generations of Adam" (Genesis 5:1) — Ben Azzai says: This is an even greater principle than that.*[^78]

Ben Azzai grounds human dignity not in the commandment to love (which might be limited to "your neighbor") but in the creation narrative, which establishes the divine image in *all* human beings. Both sages are confirming from fittingness — each arguing that his principle is the most consonant with the tradition's deepest commitments. The debate is not about truth versus falsehood but about which truth is the most fitting organizing principle.

This is confirmation at its most philosophically mature. The question is not "Is this true?" (both propositions are) but "Is this the most adequate expression of what the whole system means?"

**Sixth: From the Expedient.** A proposition is confirmed when its practical consequences are shown to be beneficial — when following it produces good outcomes for individuals and communities.

> <span dir="rtl">מפני מה נקבעה הלכה כבית הלל? מפני שנוחין ועלובין היו</span>
>
> *Why was the halakhah established according to Beit Hillel? Because they were gentle and humble.*[^79]

This is a remarkable statement. The halakhah follows Beit Hillel not because their arguments were logically superior — the Talmud acknowledges that Beit Shammai were often sharper in argument. It follows Beit Hillel because the *consequences* of their approach — a tradition of gentleness, humility, and genuine engagement with opposing views — are better for the community. The teaching is confirmed by its fruits.

Think about this in broader terms. We often say "the proof is in the pudding" — we confirm the value of an approach by looking at what it produces. The sages formalized this instinct as a method of legal reasoning. A ruling that produces communal harmony is, all else being equal, preferable to one that produces communal strife.

### The Asmakhta: A Distinctively Rabbinic Mode of Confirmation

Before we leave confirmation, you need to understand a mode of confirmation that appears to be unique to the rabbinic tradition: the *asmakhta*, or scriptural support for a ruling known to be of rabbinic rather than biblical origin.

> <span dir="rtl">אסמכתא בעלמא</span>
>
> *It is merely a support, not a genuine derivation.*[^80]

The asmakhta does not claim that the teaching originates in Scripture. It claims only that Scripture lends the teaching consonance — that the sacred text and the rabbinic ruling point in the same direction. This is confirmation from consistency, but with a crucial qualification: the relationship between text and ruling is supportive rather than generative.
